class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Madison_State_University.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nAn early illustration of the campus, from the 1885 edition of the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Blue_Book" title="Wisconsin Blue Book">Wisconsin Blue Book</a>.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The <b>University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison</b> (also known as <b>University of Wisconsin</b>, <b>Wisconsin</b>, <b>UW</b>, or regionally as <b>UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison</b>, or simply <b>Madison</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Public_university" title="Public university">public</a> <a href="/wiki/Research_university" title="Research university">research university</a> in <a href="/wiki/Madison,_Wisconsin" title="Madison, Wisconsin">Madison, Wisconsin</a>, United States. Founded when <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a> achieved <a href="/wiki/U.S._State" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. State">statehood</a> in 1848, UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison is the official state university of Wisconsin, and the flagship campus of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin_System" title="University of Wisconsin System">University of Wisconsin System</a>. It was the first public university established in Wisconsin and remains the oldest and largest public university in the state. It became a <a href="/wiki/Land-grant_university" title="Land-grant university">land-grant institution</a> in 1866.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The 933-acre (378&#160;ha) main campus includes four <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmarks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>\n<p>UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison is organized into 20 schools and colleges, which enrolled 29,536 undergraduate and 13,802 graduate students, and granted 6,902 bachelor\'s, 2,134 master\'s and 1,506 doctorate degrees in 2014\xe2\x80\x932015.<sup id="cite_ref-Community_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Community-4">[4]</a></sup> The University employs over 21,600 faculty and staff.<sup id="cite_ref-Community_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Community-4">[4]</a></sup> Its comprehensive academic program offers 136 undergraduate majors, along with 148 master\'s degree programs and 120 <a href="/wiki/Doctorate" title="Doctorate">doctoral</a> programs.<sup id="cite_ref-Academics_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Academics-3">[3]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The UW is one of America\'s <a href="/wiki/Public_Ivy" title="Public Ivy">Public Ivy</a> universities,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> which refers to top public universities in the United States capable of providing a collegiate experience comparable with the <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a>. UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison is also categorized as a Doctoral University with the Highest Research Activity in the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Classification_of_Institutions_of_Higher_Education" title="Carnegie Classification of Institutions of Higher Education">Carnegie Classification of Institutions of Higher Education</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Carnegie_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie-9">[9]</a></sup> In 2012, it had research expenditures of more than $1.1 billion, the third highest among universities in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Research_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Research-10">[10]</a></sup> Wisconsin is a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Universities" title="Association of American Universities">Association of American Universities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Badgers" title="Wisconsin Badgers">Wisconsin Badgers</a> compete in 25 intercollegiate sports in the <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_I" title="NCAA Division I">NCAA Division I</a> <a href="/wiki/Big_Ten_Conference" title="Big Ten Conference">Big Ten Conference</a> and have won 28 <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Badgers#National_championships" title="Wisconsin Badgers">national championships</a>.</p>\n<div class="toclimit-3">\n<div Mosse Humanities Building</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Van_Hise_Hall"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">Van Hise Hall</span></a></li>\n<li life</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-35"><a href="#Media"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Media</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-36"><a Life</i></span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#Notable_alumni_and_people"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Notable alumni and people</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bascom_Hall_Fire_1917.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Bascom_Hall_Fire_1917.JPG/220px-Bascom_Hall_Fire_1917.JPG" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Bascom_Hall_Fire_1917.JPG/330px-Bascom_Hall_Fire_1917.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Bascom_Hall_Fire_1917.JPG/440px-Bascom_Hall_Fire_1917.JPG 2x" data-file-width="538" data-file-height="354" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bascom_Hall_Fire_1917.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nBascom Hall fire that destroyed the dome in 1916<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bascom_Hall_at_dusk.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Bascom_Hall_at_dusk.JPG/220px-Bascom_Hall_at_dusk.JPG" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Bascom_Hall_at_dusk.JPG/330px-Bascom_Hall_at_dusk.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Bascom_Hall_at_dusk.JPG/440px-Bascom_Hall_at_dusk.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="1280" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bascom_Hall_at_dusk.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nBascom hall at dusk</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The university had its official beginnings when the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Territory" title="Wisconsin Territory">Wisconsin Territorial Legislature</a> in its 1838 session passed a law incorporating a "<b>University of the Territory of Wisconsin</b>", and a high-ranking <a href="/wiki/Board_of_visitors" class="mw-redirect" title="Board of visitors">Board of Visitors</a> was appointed. However, this body (the predecessor of the U.W. <a href="/wiki/Governing_boards_of_colleges_and_universities_in_the_United_States" title="Governing boards of colleges and universities in the United States">board of regents</a>) never actually accomplished anything before <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a> was incorporated as a state in 1848.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="Wisconsin Constitution">Wisconsin Constitution</a> provided for "the establishment of a state university, at or near the seat of state government..." and directed by the state legislature to be governed by a <a href="/wiki/Higher_education_governing_board#Regents" class="mw-redirect" title="Higher education governing board">board of regents</a> and administered by a <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_(education)" title="Chancellor (education)">Chancellor</a>. On July 26, 1848, <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Dewey" title="Nelson Dewey">Nelson Dewey</a>, Wisconsin\'s first <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Wisconsin" title="Governor of Wisconsin">governor</a>, signed the act that formally created the University of Wisconsin.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Hiram_Lathrop" title="John Hiram Lathrop">John H. Lathrop</a> became the university\'s first chancellor, in the fall of 1849.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> With <a href="/wiki/John_Whelan_Sterling" title="John Whelan Sterling">John W. Sterling</a> as the university\'s first professor (mathematics), the first class of 17 students met at <a href="/wiki/Madison_Female_Academy" title="Madison Female Academy">Madison Female Academy</a> on February 5, 1849. A permanent campus site was soon selected: an area of 50 acres (20.2&#160;ha) "bounded north by Fourth lake, east by a street to be opened at right angles with King street", [later State Street] "south by Mineral Point Road (University Avenue), and west by a carriage-way from said road to the lake." The regents\' building plans called for a "main edifice fronting towards the Capitol, three stories high, surmounted by an observatory for astronomical observations."<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> This building, University Hall, now known as <a href="/wiki/Bascom_Hill" title="Bascom Hill">Bascom Hall</a>, was finally completed in 1859. On October 10, 1916, a fire destroyed the building\'s dome, which was never replaced. <a href="/wiki/North_Hall,_University_of_Wisconsin" class="mw-redirect" title="North Hall, University of Wisconsin">North Hall</a>, constructed in 1851, was actually the first building on campus. In 1854, Levi Booth and Charles T. Wakeley became the first graduates of the university, and in 1892 the university awarded its first PhD to future <a href="/wiki/University_president" class="mw-redirect" title="University president">university president</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Van_Hise" title="Charles R. Van Hise">Charles R. Van Hise</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Wisconsin_Idea">The Wisconsin Idea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: The Wisconsin Idea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Research, teaching, and service at the UW is influenced by a tradition known as "the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Idea" title="Wisconsin Idea">Wisconsin Idea</a>", first articulated by UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison President <a href="/wiki/Charles_Van_Hise" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Van Hise">Charles Van Hise</a> in 1904, when he declared "I shall never be content until the beneficent influence of the University reaches every home in the state."<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The Wisconsin Idea holds that the boundaries of the university should be the boundaries of the state, and that the research conducted at UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison should be applied to solve problems and improve health, quality of life, the <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environment</a>, and agriculture for all citizens of the state. The Wisconsin Idea permeates the university\'s work and helps forge close working relationships among university faculty and students, and the state\'s industries and government.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Based in Wisconsin\'s populist history, the Wisconsin Idea continues to inspire the work of the faculty, staff, and students who aim to solve real-world problems by working together across disciplines and demographics.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>During World War II, University of Wisconsin was one of 131 colleges and universities nationally that took part in the <a href="/wiki/V-12_Navy_College_Training_Program" title="V-12 Navy College Training Program">V-12 Navy College Training Program</a> which offered students a path to a Navy commission.<sup id="cite_ref-wisconsin-v-12_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wisconsin-v-12-21">[21]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion">Expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin_System" title="University of Wisconsin System">University of Wisconsin System</a></div>\n<p>Over time, additional campuses were added to the university. The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Milwaukee" title="University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Milwaukee">University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Milwaukee</a> was created in 1956, and UW\xe2\x80\x93Green Bay and UW\xe2\x80\x93Parkside in 1968. Ten freshman-sophomore centers were also added to this system.<sup id="cite_ref-Wisconsin_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wisconsin-22">[22]</a></sup> In 1971, Wisconsin legislators passed a law merging the University of Wisconsin with the nine universities and four freshman-sophomore branch campuses of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin_System#Former_Wisconsin_State_Universities" title="University of Wisconsin System">Wisconsin State Universities System</a>, creating the University of Wisconsin System and bringing the two higher education systems under a single board of regents.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_activism">Student activism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Student activism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sterling_Hall_bombing" title="Sterling Hall bombing">Sterling Hall bombing</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bascom_Hill_crosses2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Bascom_Hill_crosses2.jpg/220px-Bascom_Hill_crosses2.jpg" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Bascom_Hill_crosses2.jpg/330px-Bascom_Hill_crosses2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Bascom_Hill_crosses2.jpg/440px-Bascom_Hill_crosses2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1046" data-file-height="840" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bascom_Hill_crosses2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Bascom_Hill" title="Bascom Hill">Bascom Hill</a>, 1968, with crosses placed by students protesting the Vietnam War, and sign reading, "Bascom Memorial Cemetery, Class of 1968"</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1554_sterlingmarker.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/1554_sterlingmarker.jpg/220px-1554_sterlingmarker.jpg" width="220" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/1554_sterlingmarker.jpg/330px-1554_sterlingmarker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/75/1554_sterlingmarker.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="359" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1554_sterlingmarker.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nHistorical marker near Sterling Hall commemorating fatal 1970 bombing</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>In the late 1960s and early 1970s, UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison was shaken by a series of student protests, and by the use of force by authorities in response, comprehensively documented in the film <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_at_Home_(1979_film)" title="The War at Home (1979 film)">The War at Home</a></i>. The first major demonstrations protested the presence on campus of <a href="/wiki/Recruitment" title="Recruitment">recruiters</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Dow_Chemical" class="mw-redirect" title="Dow Chemical">Dow Chemical</a> Company, which supplied the <a href="/wiki/Napalm" title="Napalm">napalm</a> used in the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>. Authorities used force to quell the disturbance. The struggle was documented in the book, <i><a href="/wiki/They_Marched_into_Sunlight" class="mw-redirect" title="They Marched into Sunlight">They Marched into Sunlight</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> as well as the PBS documentary <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Days_in_October" title="Two Days in October">Two Days in October</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Among the students injured in the protest was current Madison mayor <a href="/wiki/Paul_Soglin" title="Paul Soglin">Paul Soglin</a>.</p>\n<p>Another target of protest was the Army Mathematics Research Center (AMRC) in Sterling Hall, which was also home of the physics department. The student newspaper, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Cardinal" title="The Daily Cardinal">The Daily Cardinal</a></i>, published a series of investigative articles stating that AMRC was pursuing research directly pursuant to <a href="/wiki/US_Department_of_Defense" class="mw-redirect" title="US Department of Defense">US Department of Defense</a> requests, and supportive of military operations in Vietnam. AMRC became a magnet for demonstrations, in which protesters chanted "U.S. out of Vietnam! Smash Army Math!"</p>\n<p>On August 24, 1970, near 3:40&#160;am, a bomb exploded next to Sterling Hall, aimed at destroying the Army Math Research Center.<sup id="cite_ref-Stucki_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stucki-25">[25]</a></sup> Despite the late hour, a <a href="/wiki/Postdoctoral_researcher" title="Postdoctoral researcher">post doctoral physics researcher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fassnacht" title="Robert Fassnacht">Robert Fassnacht</a>, was in the lab and was killed in the explosion. The physics department was severely damaged, while the intended target, the AMRC, was scarcely affected. <a href="/wiki/Karleton_Armstrong" class="mw-redirect" title="Karleton Armstrong">Karleton Armstrong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dwight_Armstrong" title="Dwight Armstrong">Dwight Armstrong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/David_Fine" title="David Fine">David Fine</a> were found responsible for the blast. <a href="/wiki/Leo_Burt" title="Leo Burt">Leo Burt</a> was identified as a suspect, but was never apprehended or tried.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline_of_notable_events">Timeline of notable events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Timeline of notable events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Notable historical moments in the first 150 years of the University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison include:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>1863 Female students first admitted to University of Wisconsin during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1866 State legislature designated the University as the Wisconsin land-grant institution</li>\n<li>1888 Science Hall is constructed, one of the world\'s first buildings to use <a href="/wiki/I-beam" title="I-beam">I-beams</a><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></li>\n<li>April 4, 1892 The first edition of the student-run <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Cardinal" title="The Daily Cardinal">The Daily Cardinal</a></i> was published</li>\n<li>1894 State Board of Regents rejected an effort to purge Professor <a href="/wiki/Richard_T._Ely" title="Richard T. Ely">Richard T. Ely</a> for supporting striking printers, issuing the famous "<a href="/wiki/Sifting_and_winnowing" title="Sifting and winnowing">sifting and winnowing</a>" manifesto in defense of <a href="/wiki/Academic_freedom" title="Academic freedom">academic freedom</a>, later described as "part of Wisconsin\'s Magna Carta"<sup id="cite_ref-Library.wisc.edu_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Library.wisc.edu-31">[31]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1898 UW music instructors Henry Dyke Sleeper and Conner Ross Buerosse wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Varsity_(toast)" class="mw-redirect" title="Varsity (toast)">Varsity</a></i>, the university\'s alma mater<sup id="cite_ref-Library.wisc.edu_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Library.wisc.edu-31">[31]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1904\xe2\x80\x931905 UW Graduate School established</li>\n<li>1905 the University awards the first PhD in <a href="/wiki/Chemical_engineering" title="Chemical engineering">chemical engineering</a> ever granted, to <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Patterson_Watts" title="Oliver Patterson Watts">Oliver Patterson Watts</a>.</li>\n<li>1907 <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Union" title="Wisconsin Union">Wisconsin Union</a> was founded</li>\n<li>1909 <a href="/wiki/William_Purdy" title="William Purdy">William Purdy</a> and Paul Beck wrote <i><a href="/wiki/On,_Wisconsin" class="mw-redirect" title="On, Wisconsin">On, Wisconsin</a></i> the UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison athletic fight song</li>\n<li>1907\xe2\x80\x931911 The "<a href="/wiki/Single-grain_experiment" title="Single-grain experiment">Single-grain experiment</a>" was conducted by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Moulton_Babcock" title="Stephen Moulton Babcock">Stephen Moulton Babcock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edwin_B._Hart" title="Edwin B. Hart">Edwin B. Hart</a>, paving the way for modern nutrition as a science</li>\n<li>1913 Vitamin A discovered by UW scientist, Elmer V. McCollum</li>\n<li>1916 Vitamin B discovered by McCollum</li>\n<li>1919 Radio station 9XM founded on campus (Now <a href="/wiki/WHA_(AM)" title="WHA (AM)">WHA</a> (970 AM). It is the oldest continually operating radio station in the United States)</li>\n<li>1923 <a href="/wiki/Harry_Steenbock" title="Harry Steenbock">Harry Steenbock</a> invented process for adding vitamin D to milk</li>\n<li>1925 <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Alumni_Research_Foundation" title="Wisconsin Alumni Research Foundation">Wisconsin Alumni Research Foundation</a> chartered to control patenting and patent income on UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison inventions</li>\n<li>1934 The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison_Arboretum" title="University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison Arboretum">University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison Arboretum</a>, whose mission was to restore lost landscapes, such as prairies, was opened</li>\n<li>1936 UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison began an artist-in-residence program, the first ever at a university</li>\n<li>1940\xe2\x80\x931951 <a href="/wiki/Warfarin" title="Warfarin">Warfarin</a> (Coumadin) developed at UW. Named after <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Alumni_Research_Foundation" title="Wisconsin Alumni Research Foundation">Wisconsin Alumni Research Foundation</a></li>\n<li>1969 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Badger_Herald" title="The Badger Herald">The Badger Herald</a></i> was founded as a conservative student paper</li>\n<li>1970 <a href="/wiki/Sterling_Hall_bombing" title="Sterling Hall bombing">Sterling Hall bombing</a></li>\n<li>1976 Orchard Street Research Project: Jordan Ellefson, Mark Meyer, Daniel Dechant, Steve Niemuth</li>\n<li>1984 University Research Park founded to encourage technology transfer between university and businesses</li>\n<li>1988 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Onion" title="The Onion">The Onion</a></i> founded by two UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison students, Tim Keck and Christopher Johnson</li>\n<li>1998 UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison\'s <a href="/wiki/James_Thomson_(cell_biologist)" title="James Thomson (cell biologist)">James Thomson (cell biologist)</a> first isolated and cultured human embryonic stem cells</li>\n<li>2011 Wisconsin defeats <a href="/wiki/Michigan_State_Spartans" title="Michigan State Spartans">Michigan State</a> to win the first ever <a href="/wiki/2011_Big_Ten_Football_Championship_Game" title="2011 Big Ten Football Championship Game">Big Ten Football Championship Game</a>.</li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1549_siftwinnow.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/1549_siftwinnow.jpg/250px-1549_siftwinnow.jpg" width="250" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/1549_siftwinnow.jpg/375px-1549_siftwinnow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7a/1549_siftwinnow.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="317" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1549_siftwinnow.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n"<a href="/wiki/Sifting_and_winnowing" title="Sifting and winnowing">Sifting and winnowing</a>" plaque on Bascom Hall, UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison tribute to academic freedom</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison, the flagship campus of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin_System" title="University of Wisconsin System">University of Wisconsin System</a>, is a large, four-year research university comprising twenty associated colleges and schools.<sup id="cite_ref-Carnegie_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie-9">[9]</a></sup> In addition to undergraduate and <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_education" class="mw-redirect" title="Quaternary education">graduate</a> divisions in agriculture and life sciences, business, education, engineering, <a href="/wiki/Human_ecology" title="Human ecology">human ecology</a>, journalism and <a href="/wiki/Mass_communication" title="Mass communication">mass communication</a>, letters and science, music, <a href="/wiki/Nursing" title="Nursing">nursing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharmacy" title="Pharmacy">pharmacy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Social_welfare" class="mw-redirect" title="Social welfare">social welfare</a>, the university also maintains graduate and professional schools in <a href="/wiki/Environmental_studies" title="Environmental studies">environmental studies</a>, law, <a href="/wiki/Library_science" title="Library science">library</a> and <a href="/wiki/Information_science" title="Information science">information studies</a>, medicine and <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin_School_of_Medicine_and_Public_Health" title="University of Wisconsin School of Medicine and Public Health">(School of Medicine and Public Health)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_administration" title="Public administration">public affairs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Veterinary_medicine" title="Veterinary medicine">veterinary medicine</a>.</p>\n<p>The four year, full-time undergraduate instructional program is classified by the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Foundation_for_the_Advancement_of_Teaching" title="Carnegie Foundation for the Advancement of Teaching">Carnegie Foundation for the Advancement of Teaching</a> as "arts and science plus professions" with a high graduate coexistence; admissions are characterized as "more selective, lower transfer-in."<sup id="cite_ref-Carnegie_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie-9">[9]</a></sup> The largest university college, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison_College_of_Letters_and_Science" title="University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison College of Letters and Science">College of Letters and Science</a>, enrolls approximately half of the undergraduate student body and is made up of thirty-nine departments and five professional schools<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> that instruct students and carry out research in a wide variety of fields, such as <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, economics, <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>, history, <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>. The graduate instructional program is classified by Carnegie as "comprehensive with medical/veterinary." In 2008, it granted the third largest number of doctorates in the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-Carnegie_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NSFDoctorates_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSFDoctorates-33">[33]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rankings">Rankings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Rankings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="infobox" style="width: 22em">\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: center; font-size: 125%"><a href="/wiki/College_and_university_rankings" title="College and university rankings">University rankings</a></th>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="background-color: lavender; text-align: center">National</th>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">ARWU</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_ARWU_NU_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_ARWU_NU-34">[34]</a></sup></th>\n<td>21</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/Forbes_Magazine%27s_List_of_America%27s_Best_Colleges" title="Forbes Magazine\'s List of America\'s Best Colleges">Forbes</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_Forbes_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_Forbes-35">[35]</a></sup></th>\n<td>87</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_USNWR_NU_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_USNWR_NU-36">[36]</a></sup></th>\n<td>44</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Monthly" title="Washington Monthly">Washington Monthly</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_Wamo_NU_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_Wamo_NU-37">[37]</a></sup></th>\n<td>28</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="background-color: lavender; text-align: center">Global</th>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">ARWU</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_ARWU_W_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_ARWU_W-38">[38]</a></sup></th>\n<td>28</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings" title="QS World University Rankings">QS</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_QS_W_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_QS_W-39">[39]</a></sup></th>\n<td>55</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education_World_University_Rankings" title="Times Higher Education World University Rankings">Times</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_THES_W_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_THES_W-40">[40]</a></sup></th>\n<td>45</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_USNWR_W_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_USNWR_W-41">[41]</a></sup></th>\n<td>29</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="International">International</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: International">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In the 2016-17 <i><a href="/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings" title="QS World University Rankings">QS World University Rankings</a></i>, UW was ranked 53rd in the world and received five excellence stars.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> It was ranked 28th among world universities and 21st among universities in the U.S. in the 2016 <i><a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">Academic Ranking of World Universities</a></i>, which assesses academic and research performance.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education_World_University_Rankings" title="Times Higher Education World University Rankings">Times Higher Education World University Rankings</a></i> 2016-2017 placed it 45th worldwide, based primarily on surveys administered to students, faculty, and recruiters.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In 2015, UW-Madison was given a top ranking by <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report</i> as a global university based on the number of times it placed within the top 100 of 21 evaluated subjects, tied with Harvard University and the University of Toronto.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="National">National</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: National">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison was ranked 11th among national universities (with three institutions tied) by the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Measuring_University_Performance" title="Center for Measuring University Performance">Center for Measuring University Performance</a> in its 2007 report, with rankings based on objective statistics on research, faculty awards, student qualifications, and university assets. In 2007, the <i><a href="/wiki/Chronicle_of_Higher_Education" class="mw-redirect" title="Chronicle of Higher Education">Chronicle of Higher Education</a></i> reported that 57 disciplines at the UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison were in the top 10 in the U.S. in scholarly productivity, which placed it second after UC\xe2\x80\x93Berkeley in the number of top ten programs.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The UW placed 28th among national universities in <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Monthly" title="Washington Monthly">Washington Monthly</a>\'</i>s 2016 rankings, which consider community service and social mobility, as well as research productivity.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> ranked UW-Madison in 69th place on its list of top American colleges for 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Madison\'s undergraduate program was ranked tied for 44th among national universities by <i><a href="/wiki/U.S.News_%26_World_Report" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S.News &amp; World Report">U.S.News &amp; World Report</a></i> for 2017 and tied for 10th among public schools.<sup id="cite_ref-USNWR_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USNWR-49">[49]</a></sup> The same magazine ranked UW\'s graduate <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison_School_of_Business" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison School of Business">School of Business</a> tied for 27th, and its undergraduate business program tied for 19th.<sup id="cite_ref-USNWR_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USNWR-49">[49]</a></sup> Other graduate schools ranked by <i>USNWR</i> for 2017 include the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin_School_of_Medicine_and_Public_Health" title="University of Wisconsin School of Medicine and Public Health">School of Medicine and Public Health</a>, which was tied for 25th in research and 14th in primary care, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison_School_of_Education" title="University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison School of Education">School of Education</a> 4th, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin_Law_School" title="University of Wisconsin Law School">Law School</a> tied for 33rd, and the <a href="/wiki/Robert_M._La_Follette_School_of_Public_Affairs" title="Robert M. La Follette School of Public Affairs">La Follette School of Public Affairs</a> tied for 13th.<sup id="cite_ref-USNWR_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USNWR-49">[49]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison was a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Universities" title="Association of American Universities">Association of American Universities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In 2012, the school received $1.1 billion in research funding, placing it third in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Research_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Research-10">[10]</a></sup> Its research programs were fourth in the number of patents issued in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison is one of 33 <a href="/wiki/Sea_grant_colleges" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea grant colleges">sea grant colleges</a> in the United States. These colleges are involved in scientific research, education, training, and extension projects geared toward the conservation and practical use of U.S. coasts, the Great Lakes and other marine areas.</p>\n<p>The University maintains almost 100 research centers and programs, ranging from agriculture to arts, from education to engineering.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> It has been considered a major academic center for <a href="/wiki/Embryonic_stem_cell" title="Embryonic stem cell">embryonic stem cell</a> research ever since UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison professor <a href="/wiki/James_Thomson_(cell_biologist)" title="James Thomson (cell biologist)">James Thomson</a> became the first scientist to isolate human embryonic stem cells. This has brought significant attention and respect for the University\'s research programs from around the world. The University continues to be a leader in <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">stem cell</a> research, helped in part by the funding of the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Alumni_Research_Foundation" title="Wisconsin Alumni Research Foundation">Wisconsin Alumni Research Foundation</a> and promotion of <a href="/wiki/WiCell" title="WiCell">WiCell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Its center for research on <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engines" class="mw-redirect" title="Internal combustion engines">internal combustion engines</a>, called the Engine Research Center, has a five-year collaboration agreement with General Motors.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> It has also been the recipient of multimillion-dollar funding from the federal government.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In June 2013, it is reported that the <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">United States National Institutes of Health</a> would fund an $18.13 million study at the University of Wisconsin. The study will research lethal qualities of viruses such as <a href="/wiki/Ebola" class="mw-redirect" title="Ebola">Ebola</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Nile_virus" class="mw-redirect" title="West Nile virus">West Nile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a>. The goal of the study is to help find new drugs to fight of the most lethal pathogens.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 2012, UW-Madison experiments on cats came under fire from <a href="/wiki/People_for_the_Ethical_Treatment_of_Animals" title="People for the Ethical Treatment of Animals">People for the Ethical Treatment of Animals</a> who claimed the animals were abused. In 2013, the <a href="/wiki/NIH" class="mw-redirect" title="NIH">NIH</a> briefly suspended the research\'s funding pending an agency investigation. The following year the university was fined more than $35,000 for several violations of the <a href="/wiki/Animal_Welfare_Act_of_1966" title="Animal Welfare Act of 1966">Animal Welfare Act</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bill_Maher" title="Bill Maher">Bill Maher</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Cromwell" title="James Cromwell">James Cromwell</a> and others spoke out against the experiments that ended in 2014. The university defended the research and the care the animals received claiming that PETA\'s objections were merely a "stunt" by the organization.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Big_Ten_Academic_Alliance">Big Ten Academic Alliance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Big Ten Academic Alliance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The University of Wisconsin is a participant in the <a href="/wiki/Big_Ten_Academic_Alliance" title="Big Ten Academic Alliance">Big Ten Academic Alliance</a>. The Big Ten Academic Alliance (BTAA) is the academic consortium of the universities in the <a href="/wiki/Big_Ten_Conference" title="Big Ten Conference">Big Ten Conference</a>. Engaging in $10 billion in research in 2014-2015, BTAA universities provide powerful insight into important issues in medicine, technology, agriculture, and communities. Students at participating schools are also allowed "in-house" borrowing privileges at other schools\' libraries.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The BTAA uses collective purchasing and licensing, and has saved member institutions $19 million to date.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Course sharing,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Professional_development" title="Professional development">professional development</a> programs,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Study_abroad" class="mw-redirect" title="Study abroad">study abroad</a> and international collaborations,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> and other initiatives are also part of the BTAA.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="College_of_Agriculture_and_Life_Sciences">College of Agriculture and Life Sciences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: College of Agriculture and Life Sciences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The College of Agricultural and Life Sciences fulfills the UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison\'s mission as a land-grant university, which dates back to 1862, when Congress passed legislation to establish a national network of colleges devoted to agriculture and mechanics and Wisconsin received 240,000 acres of allotted federal land.<sup id="cite_ref-cals.wisc.edu_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cals.wisc.edu-65">[65]</a></sup> In 1885 the university began offering a winter course for farmers, the Agriculture Short Course, which was greatly developed and enhanced by <a href="/wiki/Ransom_Asa_Moore" title="Ransom Asa Moore">Ransom Asa Moore</a> from 1895 until 1907 and continues today as the Farm and Industry Short Course. In 1889 the university put all of their agricultural offerings under a new College of Agriculture, with W.A. Henry as dean.<sup id="cite_ref-cals.wisc.edu_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cals.wisc.edu-65">[65]</a></sup> Professors listed in the 1896 Agricultural Short Course for the College of Agriculture at the University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison listed popular professors such the Dean of the College of Agriculture, W.A. Henry (Feeds and Feeding), <a href="/wiki/S.M._Babcock" class="mw-redirect" title="S.M. Babcock">S.M. Babcock</a> (Agricultural Chemistry; Farm Dairying), <a href="/wiki/F.H._King" class="mw-redirect" title="F.H. King">F.H. King</a> (Agricultural Physics, Agricultural Mechanics, and Meteorology), E.S. Goff (Plant Life, Horticulture, and Economic Entomology), H.L. Russell (Bacteriology), J.A. Craig (Breeds: Breeding and Judging Live Stock), Wm. A. Scott (Economics of Agriculture), C.I. King (Practical Mechanics), Mr. <a href="/wiki/R.A._Moore" class="mw-redirect" title="R.A. Moore">R.A. Moore</a> (Parliamentary Procedures and Book-keeping), A.B. Sayles (Farm Dairying), Fred. Cranefield (Assistant in Green House Instruction), and the previous instructor in Veterinary Science, W.G. Clark, V.S.</p>\n<p>The building that housed the College of Agriculture was originally created in 1889 and was centered in South Hall on <a href="/wiki/Bascom_Hill" title="Bascom Hill">Bascom Hill</a> until the fall of 1903 when the first classes were held in the brand new College of Agriculture and Life Sciences building, where it has remained since.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> "The college has evolved and grown over the decades to reflect changes in the fabric of society and in the areas of knowledge that it studies. Practical studies related to crop and livestock production and farm life gradually delved deeper as scientists strove to understand the underlying biological processes. Today the college generates new knowledge about agriculture, natural resources management and protection, human health and nutrition, community development and related topics. Faculty and staff in 19 academic departments and a number of interdisciplinary programs carry out these lines of study."<sup id="cite_ref-cals.wisc.edu_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cals.wisc.edu-65">[65]</a></sup></p>\n<p>It has 12 associated research centers including the Marshfield Agricultural Research Station and research centers in Arlington among other locations in Wisconsin.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span id="Letters_&amp;_Science_Honors_Program"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Letters_.26_Science_Honors_Program">Letters &amp; Science Honors Program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Letters &amp; Science Honors Program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The L&amp;S Honors Program serves over 1300 students in the College of Letters and Science (the UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison\'s <a href="/wiki/Liberal_arts_college" title="Liberal arts college">liberal arts college</a>) with an enriched undergraduate <a href="/wiki/Curriculum" title="Curriculum">curriculum</a>. In addition to its curriculum, the program offers professional advising services; research opportunities and funding; and numerous academic, social and service opportunities through the Honors Student Organization. The Honors Program also supports several student organizations, such as the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison_Forensics_Team" title="University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison Forensics Team">University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison Forensics Team</a>.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="WISCIENCE">WISCIENCE</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: WISCIENCE">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Wisconsin Institute for Science Education and Community Engagement (WISCIENCE) is a unit that facilitates coordination of science outreach efforts across the university and works to improve science education at all levels.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Campus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aeroplane_view_of_University_of_Wisconsin,_Madison,_Wisconsin_(64127).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Aeroplane_view_of_University_of_Wisconsin%2C_Madison%2C_Wisconsin_%2864127%29.jpg/250px-Aeroplane_view_of_University_of_Wisconsin%2C_Madison%2C_Wisconsin_%2864127%29.jpg" width="250" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Aeroplane_view_of_University_of_Wisconsin%2C_Madison%2C_Wisconsin_%2864127%29.jpg/375px-Aeroplane_view_of_University_of_Wisconsin%2C_Madison%2C_Wisconsin_%2864127%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Aeroplane_view_of_University_of_Wisconsin%2C_Madison%2C_Wisconsin_%2864127%29.jpg/500px-Aeroplane_view_of_University_of_Wisconsin%2C_Madison%2C_Wisconsin_%2864127%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3297" data-file-height="2103" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aeroplane_view_of_University_of_Wisconsin,_Madison,_Wisconsin_(64127).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nOverhead view of central campus in the 1920s</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Located in <a href="/wiki/Madison,_Wisconsin" title="Madison, Wisconsin">Madison</a>, about a mile from the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_State_Capitol" title="Wisconsin State Capitol">state capitol</a>, the main campus of the university is situated partially on <a href="/wiki/Madison_Isthmus" title="Madison Isthmus">the isthmus</a> between <a href="/wiki/Lake_Mendota" title="Lake Mendota">Lake Mendota</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Monona" title="Lake Monona">Lake Monona</a>. The main campus comprises 933 acres (378&#160;ha) of land, while the entire campus, including research stations throughout the state, is over 10,600 acres (4,290&#160;ha) in area. The central campus is on an urban layout mostly coinciding with the city of Madison\'s street grid, exceptions being the suburban <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin_Hospital_and_Clinics" title="University of Wisconsin Hospital and Clinics">University of Wisconsin Hospital and Clinics</a>, and the Department of Psychiatry &amp; Clinics in the West Side research park. The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison_Arboretum" title="University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison Arboretum">University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison Arboretum</a>, a demonstration area for native ecosystems, is located on the west side of Madison. The main campus includes many buildings designed or supervised by architects <a href="/wiki/J.T.W._Jennings" class="mw-redirect" title="J.T.W. Jennings">J.T.W. Jennings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Peabody" title="Arthur Peabody">Arthur Peabody</a>. The hub of campus life is the <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Union_(Wisconsin)" title="Memorial Union (Wisconsin)">Memorial Union</a>. In 2011, <a href="/wiki/Travel%2BLeisure" class="mw-redirect" title="Travel+Leisure">Travel+Leisure</a> listed the Madison campus as one of the most beautiful college campuses in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison has its own police force, food service, hospital, <a href="/wiki/Recreation" title="Recreation">recreation</a> facilities, botanical gardens, <a href="/wiki/List_of_public_art_in_Madison,_Wisconsin" title="List of public art in Madison, Wisconsin">public artworks</a>, power facilities, and an on-campus dairy plant.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bascom_Hall">Bascom Hall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Bascom Hall">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BascomHill.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/BascomHill.JPG/220px-BascomHill.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/BascomHill.JPG/330px-BascomHill.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/BascomHill.JPG/440px-BascomHill.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BascomHill.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nBascom Hall atop Bascom Hill at the heart of the campus</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>As one of the icons on campus, Bascom Hall,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> at the top of <a href="/wiki/Bascom_Hill" title="Bascom Hill">Bascom Hill</a>, is often considered the "heart of the campus." Built in 1857, a decorative dome that once sat atop the structure was destroyed by fire in 1916. The structure has been added to several times over the years. The building currently houses the office of the <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_(education)" title="Chancellor (education)">chancellor</a> and vice chancellors. Bascom Hall is listed on the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a> as a contributing building within the Bascom Hill Historic District.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_Hall">Music Hall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Music Hall">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Musichall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Musichall.jpg/220px-Musichall.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Musichall.jpg/330px-Musichall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Musichall.jpg/440px-Musichall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Musichall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nMusic Hall</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>This Victorian Gothic building, built in 1878 and initially named Assembly Hall, was designed to house an 800-seat <a href="/wiki/Auditorium" title="Auditorium">auditorium</a>, a library, and a <a href="/wiki/Clock_tower" title="Clock tower">clock tower</a>. Dedicated on March 2, 1880, the building originally held <a href="/wiki/Convention_(meeting)" title="Convention (meeting)">conventions</a>, dances, and <a href="/wiki/Graduation" title="Graduation">commencement ceremonies</a>, along with its primary purpose of a library. After the library moved to a different building on campus, a portion of the hall was assigned to the School of Music in 1900. Shortly after renovations in the early 1900s, the building was officially named Music Hall in 1910. It remains an important music venue and is home to the university opera.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> This building also is home to the Department of <a href="/wiki/Urban_and_Regional_Planning" class="mw-redirect" title="Urban and Regional Planning">Urban and Regional Planning</a>, with part of the building being used as office space and classrooms.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="George_L._Mosse_Humanities_Building">George L. Mosse Humanities Building</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: George L. Mosse Humanities Building">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/George_Mosse" title="George Mosse">George L. Mosse</a> Humanities Building, located on Library Mall, was built in the late 1960s in the <a href="/wiki/Brutalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Brutalist">Brutalist</a> style. Although debunked, the campus myth is that the building (with its poor ventilation, narrow windows, inclined base, and cantilevered upper floors) was designed to be "riot-proof".<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Its seven floors house the history, art, and music departments. The most recent campus master plan calls for it to be demolished and replaced with two other buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Van_Hise_Hall">Van Hise Hall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Van Hise Hall">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vanhisehall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Vanhisehall.jpg/220px-Vanhisehall.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Vanhisehall.jpg/330px-Vanhisehall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Vanhisehall.jpg/440px-Vanhisehall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2419" data-file-height="1613" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vanhisehall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nVan Hise Hall seen from Linden Drive</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Van Hise Hall is home to most of the languages departments of the university<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> and the upper floors house the offices of the University of Wisconsin System\'s president and its Board of Regents. The building is often humorously touted by campus tour guides as the birthplace of the <a href="/wiki/Elvish_languages_(Middle-earth)" title="Elvish languages (Middle-earth)">Elven</a> language spoken in <a href="/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Rings_(film_series)" title="The Lord of the Rings (film series)"><i>The Lord of the Rings</i> film trilogy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>\n<p>At 241 feet and 19 stories, Van Hise is the third-tallest building in Madison and one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_educational_buildings" title="List of tallest educational buildings">tallest educational buildings</a> in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Because of its placement atop Bascom Hill it towers over the State Capitol as the building with the highest elevation in the city. Van Hise Hall was constructed in 1967 and its destruction is slated for sometime around 2025 as part of the university\'s campus master plan.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grainger_Hall">Grainger Hall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Grainger Hall">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Graingerhall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Graingerhall.jpg/220px-Graingerhall.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Graingerhall.jpg/330px-Graingerhall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Graingerhall.jpg/440px-Graingerhall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2254" data-file-height="1560" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Graingerhall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nGrainger Business Hall and Conference center</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Home of the Wisconsin School of Business, Grainger Hall was built in 1993. In 2008 it underwent a major renovation and addition to assist the 12 MBA specialization programs that were housed there.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The addition occupies the corner of Park Street and University Avenue, projecting the school\'s crest outward in a location that once housed a bank.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Grainger Hall also houses an array of student-run organizations, both undergraduate and graduate. There are major-specific organizations as well as organizations that welcome all students. Several of the clubs are Madison chapters of nationwide organizations, others are honor societies that require a minimum grade point average, while some exist simply to network with other students.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Wisconsin_Union">The Wisconsin Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: The Wisconsin Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Memorial_Union_and_quadrangle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Memorial_Union_and_quadrangle.jpg/220px-Memorial_Union_and_quadrangle.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Memorial_Union_and_quadrangle.jpg/330px-Memorial_Union_and_quadrangle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Memorial_Union_and_quadrangle.jpg/440px-Memorial_Union_and_quadrangle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="604" data-file-height="453" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Memorial_Union_and_quadrangle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Union_(Wisconsin)" title="Memorial Union (Wisconsin)">Memorial Union</a> as seen from the Library Mall on the UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison campus</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison has two <a href="/wiki/Student_activity_center" class="mw-redirect" title="Student activity center">student unions</a>. The older, <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Union_(Wisconsin)" title="Memorial Union (Wisconsin)">Memorial Union</a>, was built in 1928 to honor American World War I <a href="/wiki/Veteran" title="Veteran">veterans</a>. Also known as the Union or the Terrace, it has gained a reputation as one of the most beautiful <a href="/wiki/Student_activity_center" class="mw-redirect" title="Student activity center">student centers</a> on a university campus. Located on the shore of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Mendota" title="Lake Mendota">Lake Mendota</a>, it is a popular spot for socializing among both students and the public, who enjoy gazing at the lake and its sailboats. The union is known for the Rathskeller, a German pub adjacent to the lake terrace. Political debates and <a href="/wiki/Backgammon" title="Backgammon">backgammon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sheepshead_(game)" title="Sheepshead (game)">sheepshead</a> games over a beer on the terrace are common among students. The Rathskeller serves "Rathskeller Ale", a beer brewed expressly for the Terrace. Memorial Union was the first union at a public university to serve beer.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lake_Mendota_Madison,_WI.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Lake_Mendota_Madison%2C_WI.jpg/220px-Lake_Mendota_Madison%2C_WI.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Lake_Mendota_Madison%2C_WI.jpg/330px-Lake_Mendota_Madison%2C_WI.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Lake_Mendota_Madison%2C_WI.jpg/440px-Lake_Mendota_Madison%2C_WI.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lake_Mendota_Madison,_WI.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nHoofer Badger Sloops on Lake Mendota behind Memorial Union</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Memorial Union is home to many arts venues, including several art galleries, a movie theater, the Wisconsin Union Theater, and a craftshop that provides courses and facilities for arts and crafts activities. Students and Madison community members alike congregate at the Memorial Union for the films and concerts each week. An advisory <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendum</a> to <a href="/wiki/Renovation" title="Renovation">renovate</a> and expand Memorial Union was approved by the student body in 2006, and the university is currently undergoing the expansion.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Union South, the newer campus union, was built in 1971 to better accommodate a growing student enrollment and was demolished in 2008. A new "green" Union South, located on the site of the old union, opened April 15, 2011. It is a certified <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design</a> (LEED) gold building.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The building contains several dining options, an art gallery, a climbing wall, a bowling alley, event spaces, and a hotel.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Union" title="Wisconsin Union">Wisconsin Union</a> also provides a home for the Wisconsin Union Directorate Student Programming Board (WUD), which provides regular programs for both students and community members. One of the most well-known members of WUD is the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Hoofers" title="Wisconsin Hoofers">Wisconsin Hoofers</a>, a club that organizes outdoor recreational activities.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dejope_Hall">Dejope Hall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Dejope Hall">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dejope_Hall-front.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Dejope_Hall-front.JPG/220px-Dejope_Hall-front.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Dejope_Hall-front.JPG/330px-Dejope_Hall-front.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Dejope_Hall-front.JPG/440px-Dejope_Hall-front.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4928" data-file-height="3264" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dejope_Hall-front.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nDejope Residence Hall</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>On May 22, 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Ho-Chunk_Nation_of_Wisconsin" title="Ho-Chunk Nation of Wisconsin">Ho-Chunk Nation of Wisconsin</a> passed a resolution permitting the usage of the name "Dejope" for a new residence hall at the university. <i>Dejope</i> means "Four Lakes" in the Ho-Chunk language, and Native Americans have used this word to describe the Madison area for thousands of years.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The residence hall was planned as a symbol of the ongoing cooperative relationship between University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison and the Ho-Chunk nation and the building and its grounds contain imagery of the mounds and lakes in the area. A fire circle in front of the building contains plaques representing all 11 Native American nations in Wisconsin. Images of the four effigy mounds that are located on the campus (Observatory Hill, Willow Drive, Picnic Point and Eagle Heights) are embedded into the flooring of the building\'s main floor. An acrylic depiction of Lake Mendota is located in the conference room, and another artwork of glass and metal depicting the Four Lakes is located in the East Hall.</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Libraries">Libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Libraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:View_from_bascom_hall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/View_from_bascom_hall.jpg/220px-View_from_bascom_hall.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/View_from_bascom_hall.jpg/330px-View_from_bascom_hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/View_from_bascom_hall.jpg/440px-View_from_bascom_hall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1520" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:View_from_bascom_hall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nA view of the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_State_Capitol" title="Wisconsin State Capitol">Wisconsin State Capitol</a> from atop <a href="/wiki/Bascom_Hill" title="Bascom Hill">Bascom Hill</a>. The Mosse Humanities building is on the right, Wisconsin Historical Society (fore) and Memorial Library (rear) on the left.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison has the 12th largest research library collection in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-ARL_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ARL-89">[89]</a></sup> More than 40 professional and special-purpose libraries serve the campus.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The campus library collections include more than 8.3 million volumes representing human inquiry through all of history.<sup id="cite_ref-ARL_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ARL-89">[89]</a></sup> In addition, the collections comprised more than 101,000 serial titles, 6.4 million microform items, and over 8.2 million items in other formats, such as government documents, maps, musical scores, and audiovisual materials.<sup id="cite_ref-ARL_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ARL-89">[89]</a></sup> Over 1 million volumes are circulated to library users every year.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Memorial Library serves as the principal research facility on campus for the humanities and social sciences. It is the largest library in the state, with over 3.5 million volumes.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> It also houses a periodical collection, domestic and foreign newspapers, Special Collections,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> the Mills Music Library,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> a letterpress printing museum,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> and the UW Digital Collections Center.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Steenbock Memorial Library is the primary library for the College of Agriculture and Life Sciences, School of Human Ecology, School of Veterinary Medicine, UW-Extension and Cooperative Extension, and Zoology and Botany Departments. The University of Wisconsin\xe2\x80\x93Madison Archives and Records Management Department and Oral History Program are also located in Steenbock Library. The library is named for UW professor <a href="/wiki/Harry_Steenbock" title="Harry Steenbock">Harry Steenbock</a> (1886\xe2\x80\x931967), who developed an inexpensive method of enriching foods with Vitamin D in the 1920s. This library is open to the public.</p>\n<p>Undergraduates can find many of the resources they need at College Library in Helen C. White Hall.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Special collections there include Ethnic Studies, Career, Women\'s, and Gaus (Poetry). The Open Book collection, created to support the extra-academic interests of undergraduates, contains DVDs, audio books, and video games, and paperback books.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The library also has a coffee shop, the Open Book Caf\xc3\xa9.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> College Library houses a media center with over 200 computer workstations, DV editing stations, scanners, poster printing, and equipment checkout (including laptops, digital cameras, projectors, and more).</p>\n<p>The Kurt F. Wendt Library<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> serves the College of Engineering<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> and the Departments of Computer Sciences,<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Statistics,<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> and Atmospheric &amp; Oceanic Sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> In addition to books, journals, and standards, Wendt Library houses over 1.5 million technical reports in print and microfiche. Designated a Patent and Trademark Depository Library, it maintains all U.S. utility, design, and plant patents, and provides reference tools and assistance for both the general public and the UW\xe2\x80\x93Madison community.</p>\n<p>Ebling Library for the Health Sciences is located in the Health Sciences Learning Center. It opened in 2004 after the Middleton Library, Weston Library, and Power Pharmaceutical Library merged collections and staff.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The LGBT Campus Center, located in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin_Armory_and_Gymnasium" title="University of Wisconsin Armory and Gymnasium">Red Gym</a>, functions as a library for queer-themed fictional and non-fiction and provides training and resources for the entire campus.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The Kohler Art Library is located in the Conrad A. Elvehjem Building across from the Chazen Museum of Art and serves as the main campus resource for art and architecture. The library supports the Departments of Art and Art History as well as the Chazen Museum. Its collections number over 185,000 volumes covering global art movements of all periods.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-108">[108]</a></sup> A feature of the library is the Artists\' Book Collection, which contains over 1,000 artists\' books from 175 presses and artists.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-108">[108]</a></sup> The collection, created as a teaching resource in 1970 by founding Kohler Art Library Director William C.
